    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="1d2774da7bf94194b4a60c9ae19a1937">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '070cb1ff-d657-4e0a-99ce-3fb5c2834b07'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:gp-gepeng@csu.edu.cn"><span>gp-gepeng@csu.edu.cn</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Ge/Peng">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>
         </div>
      </div>
   </div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d78390494" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Fast-charging ability of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) is mainly determined by a highly effective redox reaction rate. However, traditional metal@carbon composites rarely achieve atom-level dispersion at high density, resulting in poor reaction rates. Herein, supported by the introduction of carbon vacancies, abundant C─S/C─O chemical bonds are successfully established in a carbon carrier. Then, plenty of Sb single atoms (Sb SA/PC) are first anchored with a high loading of 31.4 wt%, achieving a high yield of 210.56&nbsp;g per batch. Benefiting from dense Sb─O─C/Sb─S─C interfacial chemical bonds, Sb─S<sub>2</sub>O coordination configurations are firmly confined in the interior of carbon. Surprisingly, the conductivity of Sb SA/PC-2 is approximately 2.2 × 10<sup>7</sup> times greater than that of pristine Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. Consequently, Sb SA/PC-2 exhibits ultrahigh fast-charging capability (201.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and ultralong cycling life (364.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 5000 cycles). Theoretical calculations reveal that the fast-charging capability can be attributed to the low migration barrier and increased number of active sites. Moreover, owing to the structural integrity of Sb single atoms, phase separation is effectively inhibited. This work is anticipated to open an avenue toward designing advanced electrode materials for ultrafast-charging SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202304431-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202304431-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>With the rapid popularization of electric vehicles and electric engineering machinery, the development of all-climate fast-charging rechargeable batteries has become urgent.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0001_R_d78390486e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy. Chem. Soc. Rev. Adv. Funct. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, the notorious phenomenon of lithium plating can occur in lithium-ion batteries under fast-charging or low-temperature conditions, leading to serious safety issues, especially thermal runaway.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0002_R_d78390486e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nature. Adv. Energy Mater. Small.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison, even when encountering overdischarging to 0&nbsp;V, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) still exhibit high security; that is, their safety can persist under fast-charging and low-temperature conditions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0003_R_d78390486e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The practical application of fast-charging SIBs is impeded by their low energy density and slow redox reaction rate.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0004_R_d78390486e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To overcome these obstacles, metallic materials are considered promising for developing advanced anodes, offering high energy and power density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0005_R_d78390486e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Storage Mater. ACS Nano.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is important to note that metallic anodes experience considerable volume expansion and sluggish electrochemical kinetics, which adversely affect their fast-charging capabilities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0006_R_d78390486e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nano Lett. Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The engineering of metal@carbon composites, through rational nanostructure design and carbon recombination, has emerged as the most effective approach in this context.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0007_R_d78390486e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Nano. Energy Storage Mater. ACS Nano. J. Electrochem. Soc. Int. J. Green Energy. Nano Res.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Designing nanostructures shortens carrier transport paths, and their integration with carbon effectively mitigates volume expansion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0008_R_d78390486e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Energy. Nat. Commun. Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, achieving atom-level dispersion in metal and carbon atoms, which are densely packed, remains challenging, often resulting in suboptimal electrochemical reaction kinetics.</p>
         
         <p>Single-atom materials (SAMs), characterized by their abundance of active sites, demonstrate promising electrochemical activity, as evidenced in electrocatalysts.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0009_R_d78390486e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Mater. Lett.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Drawing from single-atom catalysts (SACs) experience, carbon-supported transition metal SACs are increasingly viewed as vital alternatives to Pt-based catalysts.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0010_R_d78390486e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Joule. Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> SACs, compared to conventional metal nanoparticles, show superior catalytic performance in electrochemical reactions due to their distinct advantages. Reducing metal species in catalysts to single atoms maximizes the exposure of active sites. For example, the oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) activity and selectivity are significantly improved when using multilayer graphene edge-hosted FeN<sub>4</sub> single-atom structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0011_R_d78390486e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Science.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, strong metal–support interactions (MSIs) between metal atoms and carbon can stabilize atomically dispersed metals and boost charge transfer and mass transport from carbon to metals. The reversible interfacial nickel–support bonds between atomic nickel and the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> support enable high reactivity and stability in the acetylene hydrogenation reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0012_R_d78390486e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In SIBs, the atomic dispersion of active metals on carbon carriers plays a crucial role in mitigating structural changes, particularly by reducing volume expansion during electrochemical reactions. The strong metal/carbon interfacial interactions also enhance efficient charge transfer and prevent metal atom aggregation. Furthermore, the distinct coordination configuration and electronic structure of SAMs may increase the number of active sites and lower the reaction barrier. Consequently, incorporating a carbon carrier and designing high-density SAMs emerge as promising approaches for achieving ultrafast and ultrastable sodium-ion storage.</p>
         
         <p>Due to the low mass loading and low yield, the reported SAMs were difficult to be used in SIBs. From previous reports, it could be noted that a high-temperature pyrolytic synthesis process usually results in the agglomeration of metal species.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0010_R_d78390486e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Joule. Adv. Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0013_R_d78390486e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Electrochem. Energy Rev. J. Am. Chem. Soc.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The enhancement of metal–carbon interfacial interactions is critical for stabilizing metal atoms at elevated temperatures. The formation of strong covalent bonds between metal atoms and carbon at the M–C interfaces is crucial in improving the thermal stability of densely packed metal atoms. It is important to note that the loading mass of SAMs is substantially constrained by the single-atom active sites hosted on edges or surfaces. Developing high-density bulk-hosted SAMs could significantly increase the active metal mass in SIBs. Achieving atomic dispersion of metal atoms within the carbon matrix necessitates the creation of abundant M–C interfacial bonds, which are essential for anchoring metal atoms; thus, preventing their migration and agglomeration. The presence of bulk defects in the carbon carrier triggers strong charge transfer, especially between heteroatoms and the 2p antibonding state of carbon atoms. This interaction is likely to provide more efficient anchoring sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0014_R_d78390486e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nat. Catal. Chem. Science. Nat. Commun.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the introduction of body-doping heteroatom sites is anticipated to facilitate the coordination with metal centers in preparing high-density SAMs, which are essential for enhancing the fast-charging capabilities of SIBs. Moreover, a detailed analysis of the bulk-hosted coordination configuration is important for revealing the energy storage mechanisms of high-density SAMs.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, surface-oxidized antimony sulfide (Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>) and defective carbon with abundant carbon vacancies were employed as the metal precursors and carbon carriers, respectively. Assisted by the introduction of extensive carbon vacancies, abundant C─S/C─O chemical bonds were established on the carbon carrier. Benefiting from the successful establishment of C─S/C─O chemical bonds, Sb single atoms (Sb SA/PC) were first prepared at a high density of 31.4 wt% and a high yield of 210.56&nbsp;g per batch. Notably, high-density Sb─S<sub>2</sub>O coordination configurations are firmly anchored within the carbon carrier via abundant Sb─S─C/Sb─O─C interfacial chemical bonds. Triggered by the high density of Sb single atoms and abundant interfacial chemical bonds, the electronic/ionic conductivities of Sb SA/PC are significantly enhanced, and the conductivity of Sb SA/PC-2 is improved by approximately 2.2 × 10<sup>7</sup> times compared to that of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. Surprisingly, Sb SA/PC-2 could deliver a capacity of 201.7&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> even at a high current density of 20 A g<sup>−1</sup> and maintain a capacity of 364.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even after cycling at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 5000 cycles, with a capacity retention of 104.4%, revealing its ultrafast and ultrastable sodium-ion storage capability in fast-charging SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202304431-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202304431-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202304431-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202304431-sec-0030-title">2.1 Synthesis and Characterization of the Physicochemical Properties of Sb SA/PC</h3>
            
            <p>A schematic diagram for the preparation of Sb SA/PC is shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0001">1a</a>. Initially, purified Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles derived from natural antimony minerals were homogeneously mixed with pyrrole. Through chemical oxidation, numerous polypyrrole (PPy) spheres with abundant defects and carbonyl functional groups were generated from the rapid in-situ polymerization and peroxidation of pyrrole.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0015_R_d78390486e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Lett. Appl. NanoBioSci. Biointerface Res. Appl. Chem.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the surface of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> was also oxidized by the added oxidizing agent and subsequently adsorbed onto the PPy spheres. Subsequently, under high-temperature calcination, the PPy spheres were pyrolyzed to nitrogen-doped carbon (NC) with abundant C vacancies. In addition, the surface-oxidized Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> was transformed into molten ions capable of penetrating and diffusing toward the vicinity of the C vacancies. Carbon vacancies served as “traps” that captured S and O atoms, leading to the formation of C─S and C─O chemical bonds. This resulted in a N, S, and O co-doped paw carbon (PC). These C─S/C─O chemical bonds effectively stabilized Sb atoms, preventing their migration and agglomeration by forming dense Sb─S─C and Sb─O─C interfacial chemical bonds. This process achieved atomic dispersion of Sb single atoms. To examine the impact of metal atoms on the fast-charging capability of Sb, materials named Sb SA/PC-<i>n</i> (<i>n</i> = 1, 2, 3), with varying Sb densities, were prepared using different dosages of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0001">1b</a>) show that Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, NC, Sb SA/PC-1, and Sb SA/PC-2 exhibit amorphous characteristics, indicating that appropriate amounts of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> loaded on PC do not form crystals after high-temperature recombination. However, a distinct diffraction peak at 28.7°, corresponding to the (012) plane of Sb metal, was observed in Sb SA/PC-3. This presence of Sb metal is likely due to the thermodynamic instability of excess Sb species, which grow into crystals through particle migration and coalescence or Ostwald ripening processes, as there are insufficient C─S/C─O chemical bonds to anchor them. XRD patterns (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a>, Supporting Information) show that without pyrrole, pristine Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> transforms into Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> crystals (Sample A) after being oxidized by the oxidizing agent. While mixing pristine Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> with polymerized PPy, there appear distinct diffraction peaks of Sb metal and Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> crystals (Sample B), highlighting the importance of in-situ pyrrole polymerization in absorbing Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. This absorption significantly affects the formation of C─S/C─O chemical bonds on PC during pyrolysis.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202304431-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1a74174c-24d0-4a94-8e78-6d372a0888f2/aenm202304431-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1a74174c-24d0-4a94-8e78-6d372a0888f2/aenm202304431-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b42f84aa-6003-4ed3-a605-17f52f15a9a4/aenm202304431-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1a74174c-24d0-4a94-8e78-6d372a0888f2/aenm202304431-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202304431-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202304431" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Synthesis and characterization of the physicochemical properties of the as-prepared samples: a) schematic diagram for preparing Sb SA/PC, b) XRD patterns, c) Raman spectra, d) ESR spectra, e) FTIR spectra, f,g) pristine high-resolution Sb 3d/O 1 s and S 2p spectra of Sb SA/PC-2, i,j) high-resolution Sb 3d/O 1 s and S 2p spectra of Sb SA/PC-2 after varying durations of Ar<sup>+</sup> sputtering, and h) electrical conductivities of the as-prepared samples.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The Raman spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0001">1c</a>) display characteristic peaks of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, including S─Sb─S and S─Sb stretching vibration peaks at 148, 188, and 251 cm<sup>−1</sup>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0016_R_d78390486e674" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Mater. Chem. A. J. Mater. Chem. C.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> confirming its amorphous structure with short-range order and long-range disorder. In Sb SA/PC-1 and Sb SA/PC-2, no Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> or Sb metal stretching vibrational peaks were observed, confirming the successful preparation of Sb single atoms. In contrast, Sb SA/PC-3 shows distinct E<sub>g</sub> and A<sub>1g</sub> vibrational peaks at 108 and 147 cm<sup>−1</sup>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0007_R_d78390486e691" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Nano. Energy Storage Mater. ACS Nano. J. Electrochem. Soc. Int. J. Green Energy. Nano Res.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively, indicating sintering of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> into Sb metal at high loading ratios, aligning with XRD results. According to thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a>, Supporting Information), Sb contents in Sb SA/PC-1, Sb SA/PC-2, and Sb SA/PC-3 were 19.0 wt%, 31.4 wt%, and 41.4 wt%, respectively (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). This indicates a maximum atom-level dispersion of Sb single atoms at a density of 31.4 wt%, surpassing previously reported Sb SAMs (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). However, at 41.4% Sb content, agglomeration and crystallization of high-surface-energy Sb atoms occur, leading to Sb metal formation. Vibrational peaks around 400 cm<sup>−1</sup> in all three Sb SA/PC samples suggest successful doping of S atoms in PC, enhancing its electrical conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0017_R_d78390486e712" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Sci. China Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The C and S contents in Sb SA/PC-1, Sb SA/PC-2, and Sb SA/PC-3 were 46 wt% and 10.3 wt%, 38.4 wt% and 12 wt%, and 27.1 wt% and 16.2 wt%, respectively (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), indicating a positive correlation between Sb and S contents, implying coordination structures between Sb and S. Additionally, two broad peaks near 1360 and 1570 cm<sup>−1</sup> in NC and the three Sb SA/PC samples represent disordered carbon defects and crystalline graphitic carbon. The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value of NC was 0.853, suggesting an abundance of defects in the amorphous carbon structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0018_R_d78390486e729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. J. Am. Chem. Soc.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Interestingly, the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value increases with Sb content, indicating that Sb introduction leads to more defect sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0019_R_d78390486e741" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The nitrogen adsorption and desorption curves, as well as the pore size distribution curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), reveal that the NC exhibits a specific surface area of 16.59 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1,</sup> along with few micropores. In contrast, Sb SA/PC-1, Sb SA/PC-2, and Sb SA/PC-3 had larger specific surface areas of 28.05, 31.96, and 24.94 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, while exhibiting almost no pores. These results indicate that the anchored Sb single atoms create new active sites on the surface of Sb SA/PC. However, due to the formation of Sb metal resulting from the agglomeration of Sb single atoms, the specific surface area of Sb SA/PC-3 is smaller than that of Sb SA/PC-2. The EPR spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0001">1d</a>) reveal a great number of carbon vacancies in the NC, resulting from the absence of carbon atoms during pyrolysis. The abundance of unsaturated active sites within the π-bonds of aromatic rings, featuring uncoordinated carbon hanging bonds, plays a crucial role in the adsorption and coordination with heteroatoms. This leads to the formation of covalent bonds, which are instrumental in promoting the atomic dispersion of metal atoms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0020_R_d78390486e763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Sb SA/PC-1, a significant reduction in carbon vacancies was observed. This indicates that the unsaturated sites in the carbon carriers coordinate with S/O heteroatoms, forming C─S/C─O chemical bonds and thereby considerably reducing the number of unpaired electrons. In Sb SA/PC-2 and Sb SA/PC-3, almost no carbon vacancies were detected, suggesting that the ion loading is sufficient to coordinate with the unpaired carbon atoms in the carbon carriers, leaving virtually no lone electrons. Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) reveals characteristic absorption peaks at approximately 1450–1650 cm<sup>−1</sup> for C═C, 600–1300 cm<sup>−1</sup> for C─H, and 1700 cm<sup>−1</sup> for C═O in Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/PPy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0021_R_d78390486e782" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The presence of C═O functional groups indicates the partial peroxidation of partial PPy, and the introduction of these groups facilitates coordination with Sb atoms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0022_R_d78390486e788" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Chem. Eng. J.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0001">1e</a>, absorption peaks at approximately 950–1480 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 1500–1700 cm<sup>−1</sup> in both NC and Sb SA/PC after high-temperature pyrolysis correspond to C─N/C─O and C═C functional groups, respectively. Moreover, the distinctive absorption peaks near 610 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 770 cm<sup>−1</sup> in the Sb SA/PC spectrum indicate the formation of Sb─O─C and C─S chemical bonds, signifying that Sb atoms are probably atomically dispersed on the N, S, and O co-doped paw carbon carrier through Sb─O─C/Sb─S─C bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0023_R_d78390486e805" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The full XPS spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a</a>, Supporting Information) display characteristic peaks of C, N, and O in NC and all three Sb SA/PCs samples. These spectra also reveal distinct peaks of Sb and S in Sb SA/PC and Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. The high-resolution C 1s spectrum of Sb SA/PC-2 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a>, Supporting Information) is distinguished by four peaks at 284.3, 285.0, 286.3, and 288.9&nbsp;eV. These peaks are associated with the bonds C─S, C─C, C─O/C─N, and C═O, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0024_R_d78390486e822" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The N 1s high-resolution spectrum (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b</a>, Supporting Information) of Sb SA/PC-2 shows three peaks at 398.4, 400.5, and 402.0&nbsp;eV. These peaks are attributed to pyridinic-N, pyrrolic-N, and graphitic-N, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0025_R_d78390486e831" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater. Small Struct. Nano-Micro Lett.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the Sb 3d/O 1s high-resolution spectrum of Sb SA/PC-2 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0001">1f</a>), peaks at 539.4 and 530.0&nbsp;eV are identified as the Sb 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Sb 3d<sub>5/2</sub> orbitals. This finding suggests an average valence state of +<i>δ</i> (0 &lt; <i>δ</i> ≤ 3) for Sb, higher than the characteristic peaks of Sb metal (537.9 and 528.5&nbsp;eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0007_R_d78390486e848" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Nano. Energy Storage Mater. ACS Nano. J. Electrochem. Soc. Int. J. Green Energy. Nano Res.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peak at 531.8&nbsp;eV in the O 1s spectrum confirms the presence of Sb─O─C bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0026_R_d78390486e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano Energy.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the S 2p high-resolution spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0001">1g</a>), the peaks at 161.9 and 163.1&nbsp;eV are ascribed to the S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> orbitals of Sb─S bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0023_R_d78390486e866" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0027_R_d78390486e866" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, peaks at 163.9 and 165.1&nbsp;eV correspond to the S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> orbitals of C─S bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0028_R_d78390486e876" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Commun. Nat. Commun. J. Power Sources.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This pattern suggests the concurrent presence of Sb─S and C─S bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0029_R_d78390486e882" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peak at 167.9&nbsp;eV is linked to SO<i><sub>x</sub></i>, indicating slight surface oxidation in Sb SA/PC-2.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0007_R_d78390486e890" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Nano. Energy Storage Mater. ACS Nano. J. Electrochem. Soc. Int. J. Green Energy. Nano Res.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high-resolution spectra of Sb SA/PC-1 and Sb SA/PC-3 (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information) display similar peaks to Sb SA/PC-2. The characteristic peaks of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> at 538.8 and 529.4&nbsp;eV are attributed to the Sb 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Sb 3d<sub>5/2</sub> orbitals (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting an average valence state of +δ for Sb in amorphous Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. Additionally, peaks at 161.4 and 162.6&nbsp;eV are attributed to S<sup>2−</sup> in Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a>, Supporting Information). Extra peaks at 163.3 and 164.5&nbsp;eV belong to the S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> orbitals of S<i><sub>n</sub></i><sup>2−</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0030_R_d78390486e936" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Energy Chem.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This indicates a minor presence of polymerized sulfur, potentially exacerbating the “shuttle effect” in polysulfides. Compared to those of the pristine Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, the characteristic peaks of Sb 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Sb 3d<sub>5/2</sub> of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/PPy (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8c</a>, Supporting Information) exhibited a blue shift toward higher binding energies. This shift indicates that the surface of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> particles in Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/PPy were oxidized to higher valence states. In the S 2p high-resolution spectrum of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/PPy (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8d</a>, Supporting Information), the characteristic peaks of S<sup>2−</sup> disappeared, and there appeared the distinct characteristic peaks of SO<i><sub>x</sub></i>, confirming the surface oxidation of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>.</p>
            
            <p>Furthermore, the chemical component and valence state of the interior areas of Sb SA/PC-2 were probed by conducting XPS analyses after varying durations of Ar<sup>+</sup> sputtering. Compared to those of the untreated Sb SA/PC-2 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0001">1f</a>), the characteristic peaks of Sb 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Sb 3d<sub>5/2</sub> in the depth range of 40&nbsp;nm to 160&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0001">1i</a>) exhibited a shift of 0.2&nbsp;eV toward lower binding energies. This shift indicates that the internal Sb species remain in the ionic state (Sb<sup>δ+</sup>), while only the surface is weakly oxidized to higher valence states. Notably, the peak near 531.8&nbsp;eV could be observed at all depths, providing strong evidence for the successful establishment of dense Sb─O─C bonds in the interior of PC. The S 2p spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0001">1j</a>) reveals that as the sputtering depth increases, the S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> peaks corresponding to Sb─S and C─S bonds remain stable, while the peaks related to SO<i><sub>x</sub></i> disappear. This observation suggested that the abundant Sb─S and C─S bonds are non-oxidized and uniformly distributed in the interior of the PC. The C 1s and N 1s spectra acquired at different sputtering depths (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information) show minimal shifts toward lower binding energies. The findings suggest that the formation of dense Sb─O─C/Sb─S─C interfacial chemical bonds assists in firmly anchoring Sb single atoms within the PC matrix. This synergistic effect, combining heteroatom doping and atomic-level dispersion of Sb atoms, leads to significantly enhanced electrical conductivities in Sb SA/PC-1, Sb SA/PC-2, and Sb SA/PC-3.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0031_R_d78390486e1016" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Mater. Chem. A. Nano Res.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These conductivities are measured at 13.7, 18.2, and 14.0 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0001">1h</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Notably, the conductivity of Sb SA/PC-2 is approximately 2.2 × 10<sup>7</sup> times higher than that of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (8.3 × 10<sup>−7</sup> mS cm<sup>−1</sup>). Therefore, these Sb SA/PC materials, with their substantially increased conductivities, are poised to significantly enhance the electrochemical reaction kinetics in fast-charging SIBs.</p>
            
            <p>SEM and TEM images (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0002">2A,B</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information) reveal that Sb SA/PC-2, produced in a high yield of 210.56&nbsp;g per batch, consists of spherical particles approximately 100&nbsp;nm in diameter, a size comparable to NC spheres (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). HRTEM images and the associated selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0002">2C</a>) further confirm the amorphous nature of Sb SA/PC-2. Elemental mapping images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0002">2D</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12C1–C3</a>, Supporting Information) obtained from the point-scan, line-scan, and surface-scan modes of energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) indicate a highly uniform distribution of Sb, S, C, N, and O throughout the Sb SA/PC-2 sample. To further investigate the internal structure, Sb SA/PC-2 particles were sectioned into approximately 30&nbsp;nm thick slices using a focused ion beam (FIB). The TEM images (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information) validate the amorphous nature of the internal structure, while the elemental mapping images (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information) demonstrate the uniform distribution of the Sb single atoms within the interior of the PC. A high-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0002">2E</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information) displays numerous atomically dispersed bright spots in Sb SA/PC-2, providing strong evidence for the successful preparation of high-density Sb single atoms. Similarly, Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16–S18</a> (Supporting Information) illustrate that the morphology and structure of Sb SA/PC-1 are analogous to those of Sb SA/PC-2, both of which are amorphous Sb SAMs. Nevertheless, Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19–S21</a> (Supporting Information) demonstrate the presence of a small quantity of Sb metal in addition to amorphous Sb single atoms in Sb SA/PC-3, which is consistent with the XRD and Raman results. Moreover, pristine Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> exhibited a network-like structure (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22–S24</a>, Supporting Information) comprising amorphous particles with a diameter of approximately 20&nbsp;nm. To elucidate the mechanism responsible for the formation of high-density Sb single atoms, the microstructural evolution of Sb SA/PC-2 was analyzed. After in-situ polymerization with PPy (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25A1–A3</a>, Supporting Information), a considerable number of surface-oxidized Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> particles were tightly adsorbed onto the PPy spheres. A subsequent high-temperature treatment at 550 °C for 10&nbsp;min (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25B1–B3</a>, Supporting Information) resulted in the decomposition of PPy into carbon spheres, while some molten surface-oxidized Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> diffused into the carbon spheres, leaving behind residual Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> particles on their surfaces. A further calcination step at 600 °C for 10&nbsp;min (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25C1–C3</a>, Supporting Information) led to most of the carbon spheres displaying clean surfaces, with only a small quantity of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles remaining. Ultimately, calcining at 600 °C for 2&nbsp;h (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information) resulted in the formation of atomically dispersed Sb single atoms on the carbon spheres, completely eliminating residual Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. Elemental mapping images captured at different stages (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26–S28</a>, Supporting Information and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0002">2D</a>) vividly illustrate the evolution process of Sb single atoms, primarily involving the diffusion and infiltration of molten surface-oxidized Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> particles into PC. This effectively validates the potential of creating high-density SAMs through defect engineering and coordination design strategies.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202304431-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7f02ba9c-e5e2-4674-a34e-084876afb073/aenm202304431-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7f02ba9c-e5e2-4674-a34e-084876afb073/aenm202304431-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/85ee9bbe-7995-42ca-b104-3c1b238d15c5/aenm202304431-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7f02ba9c-e5e2-4674-a34e-084876afb073/aenm202304431-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202304431-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202304431" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The morphology and coordination structure of Sb SA/PC-2: a) SEM image, b) TEM image, c) HRTEM image and SAED pattern; d) surface-scan elemental mapping images; e) HAADF-STEM image; f) XANES spectra; g) FT-EXAFS spectra; h) first-shell EXAFS fitting curves; and coordination configuration of Sb SA/PC-2, i–k) WT-EXAFS diagram of Sb SA/PC-2, Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> reference samples.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The coordination configuration of Sb single atoms in Sb SA/PC-2 was examined using X-ray absorption fine structure (XAFS) spectroscopy. X-ray near-edge absorption spectroscopy (XANES) analysis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0002">2F</a>) showed that the Sb SA/PC-2 profile lies between that of Sb foil and Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> reference samples, indicating that Sb has a valence range of 0 to +3, consistent well with the XPS results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0001">1F</a>). In the K-space of extended X-ray absorption fine structure spectroscopy (EXAFS) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a>, Supporting Information), the profile of Sb SA/PC-2 is similar to that of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> reference sample, suggesting predominant coordination of Sb atoms with S atoms in Sb SA/PC-2. Fourier transform (FT-EXAFS) analysis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0002">2G</a>) revealed two primary peaks at around 2.02 Å and 1.32 Å in <i>R</i>-space for Sb SA/PC-2, similar to the Sb─S bond (1.99 Å) of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> reference sample and the Sb─O bond or Sb─N bond (1.53 Å) of Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> reference sample, without the signal of Sb─Sb bond (2.70 Å) of Sb foil reference sample. This confirms the atomic-level dispersion of Sb single atoms in Sb SA/PC-2. The first-shell EXAFS fitting curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0002">2H</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30–S32</a>, Supporting Information) determined the bond lengths of Sb─S and Sb─O/Sb─N in Sb SA/PC-2 as 2.54 and 1.94 Å, respectively, with coordination numbers of 2 and 1 (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, wavelet transform (WT-EXAFS) analysis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0002">2I</a>) identified the strongest signals at 7.0 and 4.0 Å<sup>−1</sup> in Sb SA/PC-2, corresponding to Sb─S and Sb─O/Sb─N bonds. The Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> reference showed only one Sb─S coordination center at 7.0 Å<sup>−1</sup>, while the Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> reference sample showed Sb─O and Sb─Sb coordination centers at 6.5 and 11.5 Å<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Based on the presence of Sb─O─C bonds in FTIR and XPS spectra, it can be inferred that the Sb single atoms are coordinated with two sulfur atoms and one oxygen atom (Sb─S<sub>2</sub>O) in the interior of PC. Therefore, it can be conclusively affirmed that the C─S/C─O chemical bonds form strong Sb─S─C/Sb─O─C covalent bonds at the Sb/S─C and Sb/O─C interfaces, essential for stabilizing high-density Sb single atoms at elevated temperatures. The complex Sb─S<sub>2</sub>O coordination configurations and Sb─S─C/Sb─O─C interfacial chemical bonds are likely to significantly enhance the redox reaction kinetics and structural stability of the atomically dispersed Sb single-atom active centers.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202304431-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202304431-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Properties and Kinetic Analysis of Sb SA/PC</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performance of the synthesized samples was thoroughly assessed as anodes in SIBs. Illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S33</a> (Supporting Information), the initial discharge and charge specific capacities of NC, Sb SA/PC-1, Sb SA/PC-2, Sb SA/PC-3, and Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, measured at a current of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, are 222.0/84.6, 568.7/330.4, 799.8/495.5, 858.3/610.7, and 1174.7/553.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The corresponding initial coulombic efficiencies (ICE) are 38.09%, 58.09%, 61.96%, 71.16%, and 47.15%, respectively. Defects such as carbon vacancies in the carbon structure result in irreversible discharge behaviors, including additional electrolyte decomposition and strong binding energy adsorption, leading to reduced reversible sodium-ion storage capacity. The specific capacities and coulombic efficiencies of Sb SA/PC-1, Sb SA/PC-2, and Sb SA/PC-3 demonstrate a progressive increase with the rising density of Sb active species on PC. This suggests that sodium-ion storage performance can be significantly enhanced by strategically loading Sb single atoms. Although Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> exhibits a high initial discharge capacity, its ICE is only 47.15%, attributed to the poor reaction reversibility of polymerized sulfur, resulting in a marked decrease in reversible capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0032_R_d78390486e1259" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Electrochim. Acta.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After five cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S34a</a>, Supporting Information), the charge/discharge electrochemical reactions of these materials show high reversibility, with coulombic efficiencies approaching 98%. However, after 200 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S34b</a>, Supporting Information), the main galvanostatic charge-discharge (GCD) plateau of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>vanishes, and its capacity retention drops to merely 7.98% of the first cycle. This significant capacity degradation, even under a low current, is due to poor electrical conductivity of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, the “shuttle effect” of polysulfides, and sluggish reaction kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0028_R_d78390486e1282" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Commun. Nat. Commun. J. Power Sources.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the GCD curve plateaus of Sb SA/PC-1, Sb SA/PC-2, and Sb SA/PC-3 are similar to those of their fifth cycle, with capacity retention rates up to 92.86%, 94.35%, and 87.83%, respectively. This indicates that problems such as volume expansion and the polysulfide shuttling effect can be effectively solved by integration with PC, leading to considerable improvement in cycling stability.</p>
            
            <p>Upon increasing the current to 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0003">3a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S35a</a>, Supporting Information), the specific capacities of Sb SA/PC-1, Sb SA/PC-2, Sb SA/PC-3, and Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> after 1000 cycles are recorded as 285.8, 412.7, 210.7, and 9.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The corresponding capacity retentions are calculated to be 103.61%, 102.97%, 38.08%, and 2.04%, respectively. These results demonstrate that Sb SA/PC-1 and Sb SA/PC-2 maintain strong cycling stability, in contrast to the poor stability of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. The analysis of GCD curves in conjunction with differential capacity versus potential (d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i>) curves over various cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0003">3b,c</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S35</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S36</a>, Supporting Information) reveals that Peak 1 and Peak 4 correspond to redox peaks associated with the conversion/reverse conversion reaction. Meanwhile, Peak 2 and Peak 3 are related to redox peaks pertaining to the alloying/dealloying reaction. Notably, during 20 to 500 cycles, the charge/discharge reaction plateaus and the corresponding redox peaks corresponding to the alloying and conversion reactions gradually diminish in Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, indicating that the poor cycling stability of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> arises from its unstable structure and irreversible electrochemical reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0027_R_d78390486e1335" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the charging/discharging plateaus and redox peaks of Sb SA/PC-1 and Sb SA/PC-2 exhibit excellent stability during 100 to 1000 cycles. These results suggest that the volume expansion of atomically dispersed Sb single atoms is effectively mitigated by PC. Moreover, the dense Sb─O─C/Sb─S─C interfacial chemical bonds not only enhance the interfacial transport efficiency but also limit the aggregation of Sb single atoms during the reaction process, ultimately leading to significant improvements in the structural stability and reaction reversibility of Sb SA/PC-1 and Sb SA/PC-2.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0033_R_d78390486e1340" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although Sb SA/PC-3 exhibited a high specific capacity after cycling for the initial 100 cycles, the corresponding plateaus and redox peaks of the alloying/dealloying reaction gradually disappeared from 100 loops to 1000 loops, and the final specific capacity was very low. Thus, it can be inferred that the overloaded Sb atoms in Sb SA/PC-3 readily agglomerate during long-term electrochemical reactions and that the presence of Sb metal exacerbates the volume expansion, contributing to the poor structural stability of Sb SA/PC-3.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202304431-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/26bf2a97-7600-474f-91fd-bbbfa602f23f/aenm202304431-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/26bf2a97-7600-474f-91fd-bbbfa602f23f/aenm202304431-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/61341b23-b211-48af-9d22-b2ddc8f2f1d1/aenm202304431-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/26bf2a97-7600-474f-91fd-bbbfa602f23f/aenm202304431-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202304431-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202304431" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Sodium-ion storage performances of the as-obtained samples: a) cycling performance at 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, b,c) d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> curves for various cycles for Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and Sb SA/PC-2 at 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, d) rate performance at stepwise current densities from 0.5 to 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, e) capacity retention rates at each current compared to initial capacities at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, f) charge/discharge curves of Sb SA/PC-2 at different current densities, g,h) GCD curves of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and Sb SA/PC-2 during various cycles at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and i) long-period CV curves of Sb SA/PC-2 at 3.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, j) cycling performance at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Then, their rate capabilities were evaluated at various current densities (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0003">3d,e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S37</a>, Supporting Information). As the current density gradually increased from 0.5 to 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the specific capacities of the materials followed the order of Sb SA/PC-3 &gt; Sb SA/PC-2 &gt; Sb SA/PC-1 &gt; Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. Notably, when the current density reached 15.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the specific capacities of Sb SA/PC-2 and Sb SA/PC-3 only exhibited slight differences, and at 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the specific capacity of Sb SA/PC-2 was almost the same as that of Sb SA/PC-3, indicating that Sb SA/PC-2 exhibited a comparable sodium-ion storage capacity to that of Sb SA/PC-3 under ultrahigh current densities. Furthermore, when comparing the capacities of these materials at each current with their initial capacities at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, Sb SA/PC-2 exhibited relatively high retention, reaching 44.4% at 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which was greater than that of Sb SA/PC-3 (34.9%), Sb SA/PC-1 (34.7%), and Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (21.0%). The charge/discharge curves at different current densities (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0003">3f</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S38</a>, Supporting Information) also confirmed that Sb SA/PC-2 exhibited the highest capacity retention. This improvement can be attributed to the enhanced conductivity and the establishment of abundant Sb─O─C and Sb─S─C interfacial chemical bonds, which greatly improve the interfacial transport efficiency and effectively mitigate volume expansion by alleviating agglomeration. When the current density gradually increased from 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> to 15.0, 10.0, 5.0, 2.0, and 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, Sb SA/PC-2 delivered specific capacities of 201.7/250.4/310.1/377.2/428.6/487.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which were significantly greater than those of Sb SA/PC-1 (100.1/133.8/173.2/219.1/264.4/310.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). These results indicate that rationally increasing the loading mass of Sb single atoms can effectively enhance the fast-charging capability of Sb SA/PC.</p>
            
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0003">3j</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S39c</a> (Supporting Information) demonstrate the long-term cycling performance of these materials at a high current density of 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Surprisingly, Sb SA/PC-2 exhibited a specific capacity of 364.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after cycling for 5000 cycles at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, with a corresponding capacity retention of 104.4%. This value is significantly greater than those of Sb SA/PC-3 (135.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), Sb SA/PC-1 (199.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), and Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (7.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). Compared to the electrochemical performances of previously reported anodes in SIBs (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information), Sb SA/PC-2 displays outstanding fast-charging capability and cycling life. This significant improvement strongly confirmed that the establishment of abundant chemical bonds enabled the creation of high-density Sb single-atom sites, thereby achieving ultrafast and ultrastable sodium-ion storage capability. The GCD curves of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> during various cycles at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0003">3g</a>) reveal poor reaction reversibility due to inadequate conductivity and sluggish kinetics. The plateaus corresponding to the alloying and conversion reactions disappear rapidly between the 20 loop and 120 loop, accompanied by severe voltage polarization. Although the initial capacity of Sb SA/PC-3 is relatively high at the beginning of cycling, its reaction platform diminishes with an increasing number of cycling (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S39b</a>, Supporting Information). The charging capacity contribution from the voltage interval of 0.01–1.1&nbsp;V at various cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S40</a>, Supporting Information) confirmed that the presence of overloaded Sb atoms and Sb metal significantly influences the structural stability during long-term cycling, resulting in a distinct decrease in the capacity for alloying reactions. In Sb SA/PC-1 and Sb SA/PC-2, both the reaction platform and capacity contribution ratios from the alloying reaction remain stable with slight voltage polarization (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0003">3h</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S39</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S40</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, Sb SA/PC-2 exhibited a greater sodium-ion storage capacity than Sb SA/PC-1, highlighting its ultrahigh fast-charging capability and ultralong cycle life realized by high-density Sb single atoms. To investigate the variation in the redox reaction during the fast-charging process, long-term cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests were conducted at a high scan rate of 3.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0003">3i</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S41</a>, Supporting Information). The redox peak currents corresponding to the conversion/reverse conversion and alloying/dealloying reactions of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> decrease rapidly with increasing scanning number, indicating aggravated volume expansion and the shuttle effect of polysulfide during fast-charging process. This phenomenon results in a significant decrease in the capacity for major redox reactions. While the current intensities of the redox peaks in Sb SA/PC-3 decrease relatively slowly, the peak current intensities of the alloying and conversion reactions dramatically decrease after hundreds of scans. These findings confirm that the presence of excessive Sb species profoundly influences the stability of the redox reactions in Sb SAMs. In contrast, both Sb SA/PC-1 and Sb SA/PC-2 exhibit minimal attenuation of the current intensities of the redox peaks even after 600 repeated cycles at a high scan rate, strongly demonstrating their superior cycling stability. Importantly, the redox peak currents of Sb SA/PC-2 are distinctly greater than those of Sb SA/PC-1, revealing that its ultrahigh rate capability may arise from accelerated electrochemical reaction kinetics.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The electrochemical kinetic parameters of the prepared samples were evaluated via CV measurements at stepwise scan rates ranging from 0.1 to 0.9&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0004">4a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S42</a>, Supporting Information). Compared to Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and Sb SA/PC-3, Sb SA/PC-1 and Sb SA/PC-2 exhibit indistinct boundaries between Peak 3 and Peak 4, probably attributed to structural integrity during electrochemical reactions, which is beneficial for preventing phase separation phenomena. The sodium-ion diffusion coefficients (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/17b4d6e7-66c7-4204-8a49-0db65ef6245a/aenm202304431-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202304431:aenm202304431-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202304431-math-0001.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>Na</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^{\mathrm{ + }}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>) for the reduction peak (Peak 1) and oxidation peak (Peak 4) corresponding to the conversion/reverse conversion reaction were calculated using the Randles-Sevcik equation (Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#aenm202304431-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>) and the fitted <i>I</i><sub>P</sub>/<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S43a</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0034_R_d78390486e1547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Mater. Nano Energy.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/5e2daed1-1ab6-458b-b963-a55db67957c7/aenm202304431-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202304431:aenm202304431-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202304431-math-0002.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>Na</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^{\mathrm{ + }}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> value (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S43b</a>, Supporting Information) for the conversion/reverse conversion reaction in Sb SA/PC-2 is greater than that of the other samples. This result demonstrated that the presence of high-density Sb single atoms and dense Sb─O─C/Sb─S─C interfacial chemical bonds significantly enhanced the interfacial sodium-ion transport rate, leading to improved kinetics and reversibility of the conversion reaction. Additionally, following Dunn's report (Equations&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#aenm202304431-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a> and&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#aenm202304431-disp-0003" title="Link to equation">3</a>), the <i>b</i> values corresponding to the largest reduction peak (Peak 1) and the largest oxidation peak (Peak 3) were obtained by fitting log(<i>i</i>)/log(<i>v</i>) curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S44</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0004">4b</a>, the <i>b</i> values of Sb SA/PC-2 (0.99/1.01) are greater than those of Sb SA/PC-1 (0.88/0.95), Sb SA/PC-3 (0.82/0.86), and Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (0.73/0.74). A <i>b</i> value greater than 0.5 suggests that the electrochemical processes in these samples are predominantly controlled by the charge-storage process occurring near the surface of the electrode material, commonly referred to as pseudocapacitive behavior. The pseudocapacitive behavior is exceptional compared to the slow diffusion behavior, so increasing the pseudocapacitive contribution can enhance the electrochemical reaction kinetics. The pseudo-capacitance contributions at different scan rates (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0004">4c</a>) were quantitatively determined by fitting the <i>i/v</i><sup>1/2</sup>/<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> curves according to Equations&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#aenm202304431-disp-0004" title="Link to equation">4</a> and&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#aenm202304431-disp-0005" title="Link to equation">5</a>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0035_R_d78390486e1609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, Sb SA/PC-2 exhibited the largest pseudo-capacitance contribution, followed by Sb SA/PC-1, Sb SA/PC-3, and Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0004">4d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S45</a> (Supporting Information), the pseudo-capacitance contribution of Sb SA/PC-2 reached 99.43% at a scan rate of 0.9&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, which is greater than that of Sb SA/PC-1 (97.03%), Sb SA/PC-3 (92.52%), and Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (92.27%). The significant increase in the pseudocapacitive behavior is considered the fundamental reason behind the ultrahigh fast-charging ability of Sb SA/PC-2.

            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202304431-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/98a3efdb-12c1-472b-b885-16fd0b6479f3/aenm202304431-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/98a3efdb-12c1-472b-b885-16fd0b6479f3/aenm202304431-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/35a5684f-6c48-40b1-b3b7-54271d52ceab/aenm202304431-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/98a3efdb-12c1-472b-b885-16fd0b6479f3/aenm202304431-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202304431-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202304431" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The electrochemical kinetics of the as-obtained samples: a) CV curves of Sb SA/PC-2 at stepwise scan rates from 0.1 to 0.9&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, b) values of Peak 1 and Peak 3, c) pseudo-capacitance contributions at different scan rates, d) pseudo-capacitance contribution of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and Sb SA/PC-2 at 0.9&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, e) GITT curves and the corresponding <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/1912e307-8f2a-4b65-88e4-43e45acf6c84/aenm202304431-math-0008.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202304431:aenm202304431-math-0008" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202304431-math-0008.png">
