            <div class="paragraph-element">To further investigate the sodium-ion diffusion kinetics during the electrochemical reaction, sodium-ion diffusion coefficients for different sodiation and desodiation periods were determined using the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) according to Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#aenm202304431-disp-0006" title="Link to equation">6</a> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0004">4e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S46</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0036_R_d78390486e1835" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Energy Chem. Energy Storage Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared to those for Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, the <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f19d0a86-7045-49ef-a259-0115eaadf328/aenm202304431-math-0009.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202304431:aenm202304431-math-0009" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202304431-math-0009.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>Na</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^{\mathrm{ + }}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> values for Sb SA/PC 1–3, particularly for Sb SA/PC-2, were significantly greater during the entire redox reaction. This enhancement suggested accelerated alloying/dealloying and conversion/reverse conversion reactions, thereby improving the redox reaction kinetics and reversibility. Furthermore, in situ electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was performed to investigate the changes in impedance during the electrochemical reaction at various charging and discharging voltages (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0004">4f,g</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S47</a>, Supporting Information). The impedance values of Sb SA/PC 1-3 are consistently lower than those of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> throughout the charging and discharging process. The reduced impedance can be attributed to the significantly enhanced conductivity and promoted charge transfer facilitated by the abundant Sb─O─C/Sb─S─C interfacial chemical bonds. Notably, Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and Sb SA/PC-3 exhibited tremendous volume expansions during discharge, resulting in a prominent increase in impedance. The high impedances hindered the charge transfer rate in the redox reaction and affected the fast-charging capacity. Conversely, the impedance values of Sb SA/PC-1 and Sb SA/PC-2 only slightly increased during discharge, indicating that dense Sb─O─C/Sb─S─C interfacial bonds mitigated volume expansion by restricting the aggregation of highly dispersed Sb single atoms during the electrochemical reaction. After cycling for various cycles, the impedances of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and Sb SA/PC-3 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0004">4h,i</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S48</a>, Supporting Information) exhibited an enormous increase, resulting from the separation of the Sb and S phases caused by volume expansion, the “shuttle effect” of polysulfide, and even pulverization or detachment during long-term cycling. In contrast, the impedances of Sb SA/PC-1 and Sb SA/PC-2 did not noticeably change, indicating ultrastrong structural integrity. The charge transfer impedance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) obtained by the fitted equivalent circuit model (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S49</a>, Supporting Information) demonstrated that the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values for Sb SA/PC-2 at the 30<sup>th</sup> and 500<sup>th</sup> cycles were 25.3 and 21.9 Ω, respectively, which are significantly smaller than those of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (84.6 Ω and 675.6 Ω). This confirms that due to the robust structural stability of Sb SA/PC-2, phase separation was effectively prevented even after numerous cycles.

               <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202304431-disp-0006"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202304431-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202304431-sec-0050-title">2.3 Theoretical Calculations and Structural Evolution of Sb SA/PC-2 after Cycling</h3>
            
            <p>First-principles calculations were utilized to explore the atomic-level fast-charging mechanism in Sb SA/PC-2 during electrochemical reactions. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S50</a> (Supporting Information) presents the optimized geometrical configurations of NC, Sb SA/PC, and Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. NC is characterized by N-doped hexatomic ring carbon structures with carbon vacancies. Sb SA/PC features Sb single atoms, established through Sb─S<sub>2</sub>O coordination structures, anchored in a carrier of N, S, and O co-doped paw carbon. Additionally, Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> is formed from a layered structure via the stacking of 1D (Sb<sub>4</sub>S<sub>6</sub>)<i><sub>n</sub></i> chains. The density of states (DOS) analysis shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0005">5a</a> indicates that Sb SA/PC, equipped with the Sb─S<sub>2</sub>O coordination structure, exhibits the highest electron density at the Fermi energy level, suggesting ultrahigh electrical conductivity. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0005">5b</a> depicts the optimized configurations of sodium-ion adsorbed on these three materials. The adsorption energies for Sb SA/PC, Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, and NC are −3.21, −2.46, and −1.66&nbsp;eV, respectively. This demonstrates that Sb SA/PC offers additional adsorption sites and strong sodium-ion adsorption capabilities. Furthermore, the sodium-ion diffusion energy barrier, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0005">5c</a>, is lower for Sb SA/PC (0.389&nbsp;eV) compared to Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (0.669&nbsp;eV) and NC (0.543&nbsp;eV). This finding confirms that the Sb─S<sub>2</sub>O coordination structure significantly reduces the reaction barrier and improves sodium-ion diffusion kinetics. The differential charge density (DCD) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0005">5d</a>) shows that NC exhibits symmetric regions for electron transfer with a small quantity of electron transfer. In contrast, compared with Sb SA/PC, which has a Sb─S<sub>2</sub>O coordination structure, Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> has more sites and more electrons transferred, while Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> has a smaller electron transfer region. These results highlight that enhancing the number of active sites is highly important for improving the fast-charging capability of Sb SA/PC.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202304431-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/28daf4c2-bf60-476f-95fd-057f49ee8904/aenm202304431-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/28daf4c2-bf60-476f-95fd-057f49ee8904/aenm202304431-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0c6e96e1-f38e-43d3-a89d-9d622e16a2f2/aenm202304431-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/28daf4c2-bf60-476f-95fd-057f49ee8904/aenm202304431-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202304431-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202304431" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Theoretical calculations and structural evolution after cycling of the as-obtained samples: a) DOS of NC, Sb SA/PC, and Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>; b) the adsorption energies of sodium-ion adsorption on the three samples; c) the sodium-ion diffusion energy barriers of the three samples; d) DCD of the optimized configurations of sodium-ion adsorption on the three samples; e) the formation energies of multi-step sodium-ion insertion in Sb SA/PC; f) ex situ XPS at different potentials of Sb SA/PC-2 after cycling for 100 cycles; g) cycling performances at 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> of the full cell assembled with Sb SA/PC-2&nbsp;versus NVP.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The energy storage mechanism of Sb SA/PC was proposed based on the formation energy of the multi-step sodium-ion insertion (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0005">5e</a>). In the initial stage, the adsorption reaction involves the breaking of dense C─O─Sb and Sb─S─C interfacial chemical bonds as the voltage decreases, resulting in unsaturated C─O─ and C─S─ active sites. The active sites in the system, including nitrogen-doped sites, effectively facilitate the adsorption of sodium-ion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0037_R_d78390486e2024" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The process begins with a conversion reaction, where two Sb─S bonds dissociate from the carbon carrier and react with two sodium-ions, resulting in the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>S. In the subsequent alloying reaction, the remaining Sb single atoms react with three sodium-ions to form Na<sub>3</sub>Sb. After three electrochemical stages involving ten sodium-ions, the formation energy of the system approaches 0 eV, making it challenging to bind an additional, eleventh sodium ion. Notably, the formation energies for the alloying reaction are relatively low, attributed to the rapid alloying of highly unsaturated Sb atoms with sodium-ion following the disruption of Sb─S bonds. During the dealloying reaction in the charging process, the active Sb single atoms, derived from the desodiation of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, quickly recombine with S and O to recreate the saturated Sb─S<sub>2</sub>O coordination structure. Thus, the high reactivity of Sb single atoms contributes to enhanced electrochemical kinetics and reaction reversibility, effectively mitigating phase separation issues.</p>
            
            <p>To assess the structural stability of Sb SA/PC-2 after numerous cycling, ex situ XPS and ex situ TEM analyses were conducted at different potentials following 100 cycles. After etching with Ar<sup>+</sup> for 60 s to remove the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0005">5f</a>), discharging to 0.01&nbsp;V leads to a redshift in the high-resolution Sb 3d/O1s and S 2p spectra of Sb SA/PC-2, indicative of electron gaining during the reduction reaction. The peaks at 538.4 and 529.0&nbsp;eV in the Sb 3d/O1s spectrum are characteristic of the Sb 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Sb 3d<sub>5/2</sub> peaks of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0038_R_d78390486e2053" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Mater. Chem. A.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peak at 535.3&nbsp;eV is identified as the characteristic peak of Na Auger, while the peak at 530.7&nbsp;eV suggests the formation of C─O─Na bonds, resulting from the adsorption of sodium-ion by disrupted Sb─O─C bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0039_R_d78390486e2058" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Electrochim. Acta. ACS Nano.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the S 2p spectrum, the peaks at 160.3 and 161.5&nbsp;eV correspond to the S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>S, respectively, and the peaks at 162.3 and 163.5&nbsp;eV indicate the formation of C─S─Na bonds due to sodium-ion adsorption by broken Sb─S─C bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0040_R_d78390486e2070" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These discharged products align well with the proposed sodium-ion storage mechanism depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0005">5e</a>. Upon recharging to 2.5&nbsp;V, the discharged products lose electrons, and the characteristic peaks revert to their original positions, along with the regeneration of Sb─S bonds following the desodiation of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and Na<sub>2</sub>S, and the reformation of Sb─S─C/Sb─O─C interfacial chemical bonds after the desodiation of C─S─Na and C─O─Na.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0037_R_d78390486e2083" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">37</a>, <a href="#aenm202304431-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202304431-bib-0041_R_d78390486e2083" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Mater. Chem. A.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These results demonstrate the ultrahigh reversibility and stability of the dense Sb─S─C/Sb─O─C interfacial chemical bonds even after numerous cycles, effectively preventing the “shuttle effect” of polysulfides.</p>
            
            <p>After undergoing 100 cycles at a current of 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the disassembled electrode and separator of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S51A1</a>,&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">A2</a>, Supporting Information) showed clear signs of detachment of the active materials and severe dissolution of polysulfides. This indicates an aggravated phase separation of Sb and S in the Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> electrode. In contrast, Sb SA/PC-2 displayed a completely intact electrode and a clean separator (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S51B1</a>,&nbsp; <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">B2</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting a well-maintained integrated phase and structural stability. Ex-situ TEM images and SAED pattern of Sb SA/PC-2 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S52</a>, Supporting Information) demonstrate that it retains its original amorphous nanosphere morphology even after cycling at 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 100 cycles. The elemental mapping images (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S53</a>, Supporting Information) show a consistent distribution of Sb, S, O, C, and N elements across the material, confirming the persistence of highly dispersed Sb single atoms and the absence of phase separation, thanks to the robust structural stability of Sb SA/PC-2. In stark contrast, Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> was observed to have formed large particles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S54</a>, Supporting Information), aggregated by polycrystalline Sb metal. This aggregation is attributed to the phase separation phenomenon resulting from substantial volume expansion of the Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles during the electrochemical reactions. The difference in behavior between Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and Sb SA/PC-2 underlines the superior structural stability and phase integrity of Sb SA/PC-2, making it a more suitable material for high-performance sodium-ion batteries. Notably, the elemental mapping images of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S55</a>, Supporting Information) show some regions containing Sb but lacking S, suggesting the dissolution and loss of polysulfide species. These findings validate the poor structural stability of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> caused by the phase separation of Sb and S during the electrochemical reaction process. Furthermore, the low-temperature electrochemical performance of Sb SA/PC-2 was evaluated. At a low temperature of −5 °C (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S56</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">57</a>, Supporting Information), Sb SA/PC-2 exhibited specific capacities of 423.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 360.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after cycling for 300 cycles at 0.1 and 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, highlighting the rapid reaction rate even at low temperatures. Moreover, a sodium-ion pouch cell assembled with Sb SA/PC-2 and sodium vanadium phosphate (NVP) as the anode and cathode, respectively, can successfully illuminate a CSU logo pattern composed of LED bulbs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S58</a>, Supporting Information). After cycling at a high current density of 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 100 cycles, the specific capacity of the full cell still remains at 338.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating its potential for use in advanced fast-charging SIBs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202304431-fig-0005">5g</a>). Therefore, supported by ultrafast reaction kinetics and ultrahigh structural stability, the fast-charging capability and long-term cycling life of Sb SA/PC-2 are significantly improved.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202304431-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202304431-sec-0060-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>Capitalizing on the introduction of carbon vacancies and the further development of C─S/C─O chemical bonds in the carbon carrier, a high concentration of Sb single atoms (31.4 wt%) is first anchored in the carbon, yielding an impressive 210.56&nbsp;g per batch. This process is supported by the formation of extensive Sb─S─C/Sb─O─C interfacial chemical bonds, leading to the firm integration of Sb─S<sub>2</sub>O coordination configurations within the carbon matrix. As a consequence, the electronic and ionic conductivities of Sb SA/PC-2 are significantly improved, with the conductivity being notably increased by 2.2 × 10<sup>7</sup> times compared to pristine Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. Therefore, even at a high current density of 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, Sb SA/PC-2 demonstrates an impressive specific capacity of 201.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Remarkably, after 5000 cycles at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, Sb SA/PC-2 maintains a high specific capacity of 364.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 104.4%. These findings underscore the exceptional fast-charging capabilities and prolonged cycle life of the Sb SA/PC-2 composite. Supported by theoretical calculations, the impressive fast-charging capability of Sb SA/PC-2 was attributed to the low migration barrier and increased number of active sites. Ex-situ techniques demonstrate that the robust structural stability of Sb single atoms effectively prevent phase separation phenomena. This work provides a novel strategy for developing advanced anodes for promising fast-charging SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
